## Chapter 2: Wireless Channel Models:




### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 2 of "Principles of Wireless Communications: A Comprehensive Guide". In this chapter, we will delve into the fundamental concepts of modulation and coding, two crucial components of wireless communication systems.

Modulation is the process of converting digital information into analog signals for transmission over a communication channel. It is a critical step in wireless communication as it allows for the efficient transmission of data over long distances. We will explore the different types of modulation techniques, including amplitude modulation (AM), frequency modulation (FM), and phase modulation (PM), and discuss their advantages and disadvantages.

Coding, on the other hand, is the process of adding redundancy to digital data to improve its reliability during transmission. In wireless communication, coding is essential as it helps mitigate the effects of noise and interference, which are inevitable in wireless channels. We will cover the basics of coding, including block codes and convolutional codes, and discuss their applications in wireless communication.

Throughout this chapter, we will use mathematical expressions to explain the concepts of modulation and coding. For example, we might represent the modulated signal as `$y(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_ct + \phi) + A_s\cos(2\pi f_st + \phi_s)$`, where `$A_c$` and `$A_s$` are the amplitudes of the carrier and signal waves, `$f_c$` and `$f_s$` are the carrier and signal frequencies, and `$\phi$` and `$\phi_s$` are the phases of the carrier and signal waves.

By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of the principles of modulation and coding and their role in wireless communication systems. So, let's dive in and explore the fascinating world of wireless communications.




### Section: 2.1 Digital Modulation Techniques:

Digital modulation techniques are essential in wireless communication systems as they allow for the efficient transmission of digital data over a communication channel. In this section, we will explore the different types of digital modulation techniques, including amplitude modulation (AM), frequency modulation (FM), and phase modulation (PM).

#### 2.1a Amplitude Modulation Techniques

Amplitude modulation (AM) is a type of modulation where the amplitude of the carrier signal is varied in accordance with the digital data being transmitted. The mathematical representation of an AM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c[1 + m(t)]\cos(2\pi f_ct)
$$

where `$A_c$` is the amplitude of the carrier signal, `$m(t)$` is the message signal, and `$f_c$` is the carrier frequency.

AM has the advantage of being able to transmit data at a higher rate compared to other modulation techniques. However, it is susceptible to noise and interference, which can cause distortion in the received signal.

#### 2.1b Frequency Modulation Techniques

Frequency modulation (FM) is a type of modulation where the frequency of the carrier signal is varied in accordance with the digital data being transmitted. The mathematical representation of an FM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos[2\pi f_ct + \Delta\phi(t)]
$$

where `$A_c$` is the amplitude of the carrier signal, `$f_c$` is the carrier frequency, and `$\Delta\phi(t)$` is the phase deviation caused by the message signal.

FM has the advantage of being less susceptible to noise and interference compared to AM. However, it has a lower data transmission rate compared to AM.

#### 2.1c Phase Modulation Techniques

Phase modulation (PM) is a type of modulation where the phase of the carrier signal is varied in accordance with the digital data being transmitted. The mathematical representation of a PM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_ct + k_pm(t))
$$

where `$A_c$` is the amplitude of the carrier signal, `$f_c$` is the carrier frequency, and `$k_p$` is the phase sensitivity constant.

PM has the advantage of being able to transmit data at a higher rate compared to FM. However, it is more susceptible to noise and interference compared to FM.

In the next section, we will explore the different types of digital modulation techniques in more detail and discuss their applications in wireless communication systems.

#### 2.1d Quadrature Amplitude Modulation

Quadrature Amplitude Modulation (QAM) is a digital modulation technique that combines both amplitude and phase modulation. It is a form of M-ary modulation, where each symbol consists of two elements: the amplitude and the phase of the carrier signal. The mathematical representation of a QAM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_ct + k_pm(t))
$$

where `$A_c$` is the amplitude of the carrier signal, `$f_c$` is the carrier frequency, and `$k_p$` is the phase sensitivity constant. The amplitude and phase of the carrier signal are determined by the digital data being transmitted.

QAM has the advantage of being able to transmit data at a higher rate compared to AM, FM, and PM. However, it is more susceptible to noise and interference compared to these modulation techniques.

#### 2.1e Orthogonal Frequency Division Multiplexing

Orthogonal Frequency Division Multiplexing (OFDM) is a digital modulation technique that divides the available bandwidth into multiple subcarriers, each carrying a lower rate data stream. The data streams are modulated onto the subcarriers using different modulation schemes, such as Binary Phase Shift Keying (BPSK), Quadrature Phase Shift Keying (QPSK), 16-Quadrature Amplitude Modulation (16-QAM), and 64-Quadrature Amplitude Modulation (64-QAM). The modulated signals are then combined to form the transmitted signal.

OFDM has the advantage of being able to achieve high spectral efficiency and robustness against multipath fading. However, it is more complex and requires more sophisticated signal processing techniques compared to other modulation techniques.

#### 2.1f Single-Sideband Modulation

Single-Sideband Modulation (SSB) is a digital modulation technique that transmits only one sideband of the carrier signal, while the other sideband is suppressed. This results in a lower bandwidth and power requirement compared to double-sideband modulation. SSB has the advantage of being able to achieve higher spectral efficiency and lower power consumption compared to other modulation techniques. However, it is more susceptible to noise and interference compared to other modulation techniques.

#### 2.1g Multiple Frequency Shift Keying

Multiple Frequency Shift Keying (MFSK) is a digital modulation technique that uses multiple frequency shifts to represent different symbols. Each symbol is represented by a unique frequency shift. MFSK has the advantage of being able to achieve high spectral efficiency and robustness against noise and interference. However, it is more complex and requires more sophisticated signal processing techniques compared to other modulation techniques.

#### 2.1h Minimum Shift Keying

Minimum Shift Keying (MSK) is a digital modulation technique that uses a constant envelope to achieve high spectral efficiency and robustness against noise and interference. MSK has the advantage of being able to achieve high spectral efficiency and robustness against noise and interference. However, it is more complex and requires more sophisticated signal processing techniques compared to other modulation techniques.

#### 2.1i Differential Phase Shift Keying

Differential Phase Shift Keying (DPSK) is a digital modulation technique that uses the difference in phase between consecutive symbols to represent different symbols. DPSK has the advantage of being able to achieve high spectral efficiency and robustness against noise and interference. However, it is more complex and requires more sophisticated signal processing techniques compared to other modulation techniques.

#### 2.1j Quadrature Phase Shift Keying

Quadrature Phase Shift Keying (QPSK) is a digital modulation technique that uses the phase of the carrier signal to represent different symbols. QPSK has the advantage of being able to achieve high spectral efficiency and robustness against noise and interference. However, it is more complex and requires more sophisticated signal processing techniques compared to other modulation techniques.

#### 2.1k Binary Phase Shift Keying

Binary Phase Shift Keying (BPSK) is a digital modulation technique that uses the phase of the carrier signal to represent two symbols, typically 0 and 1. BPSK has the advantage of being able to achieve high spectral efficiency and robustness against noise and interference. However, it is more complex and requires more sophisticated signal processing techniques compared to other modulation techniques.

#### 2.1l Non-Coherent Detection

Non-Coherent Detection (NCD) is a digital modulation technique that does not require a phase-locked loop (PLL) to track the phase of the carrier signal. This makes it a suitable candidate for optical communications systems, where coherent phase modulation and detection are difficult and extremely expensive. NCD has the advantage of being able to achieve high spectral efficiency and robustness against noise and interference. However, it is more complex and requires more sophisticated signal processing techniques compared to other modulation techniques.

#### 2.1m Pulse-Position Modulation

Pulse-Position Modulation (PPM) is a digital modulation technique that uses the position of a pulse to represent different symbols. PPM has the advantage of being able to achieve high spectral efficiency and robustness against noise and interference. However, it is more complex and requires more sophisticated signal processing techniques compared to other modulation techniques.

#### 2.1n Multiple Frequency Shift Keying

Multiple Frequency Shift Keying (MFSK) is a digital modulation technique that uses multiple frequency shifts to represent different symbols. Each symbol is represented by a unique frequency shift. ### Section: 2.1 Digital Modulation Techniques:

Digital modulation techniques are essential in wireless communication systems as they allow for the efficient transmission of digital data over a communication channel. In this section, we will explore the different types of digital modulation techniques, including amplitude modulation (AM), frequency modulation (FM), and phase modulation (PM).

#### 2.1a Amplitude Modulation Techniques

Amplitude modulation (AM) is a type of modulation where the amplitude of the carrier signal is varied in accordance with the digital data being transmitted. The mathematical representation of an AM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c[1 + m(t)]\cos(2\pi f_ct)
$$

where `$A_c$` is the amplitude of the carrier signal, `$m(t)$` is the message signal, and `$f_c$` is the carrier frequency.

AM has the advantage of being able to transmit data at a higher rate compared to other modulation techniques. However, it is susceptible to noise and interference, which can cause distortion in the received signal.

#### 2.1b Frequency Modulation Techniques

Frequency modulation (FM) is a type of modulation where the frequency of the carrier signal is varied in accordance with the digital data being transmitted. The mathematical representation of an FM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos[2\pi f_ct + \Delta\phi(t)]
$$

where `$A_c$` is the amplitude of the carrier signal, `$f_c$` is the carrier frequency, and `$\Delta\phi(t)$` is the phase deviation caused by the message signal.

FM has the advantage of being less susceptible to noise and interference compared to AM. However, it has a lower data transmission rate compared to AM.

#### 2.1c Phase Modulation Techniques

Phase modulation (PM) is a type of modulation where the phase of the carrier signal is varied in accordance with the digital data being transmitted. The mathematical representation of a PM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_ct + k_pm(t))
$$

where `$A_c$` is the amplitude of the carrier signal, `$f_c$` is the carrier frequency, and `$k_p$` is the phase sensitivity constant.

PM has the advantage of being able to transmit data at a higher rate compared to FM. However, it is more susceptible to noise and interference compared to FM.

### Subsection: 2.1d Quadrature Amplitude Modulation Techniques

Quadrature amplitude modulation (QAM) is a type of modulation where both the amplitude and phase of the carrier signal are varied in accordance with the digital data being transmitted. The mathematical representation of a QAM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_ct + k_pm(t)) + A_c\sin(2\pi f_ct + k_pm(t))
$$

where `$A_c$` is the amplitude of the carrier signal, `$f_c$` is the carrier frequency, and `$k_p$` is the phase sensitivity constant.

QAM has the advantage of being able to transmit data at a higher rate compared to AM, FM, and PM. However, it is more susceptible to noise and interference compared to AM and FM.

### Subsection: 2.1e Single-Sideband Modulation Techniques

Single-sideband modulation (SSB) is a type of modulation where only one sideband of the carrier signal is transmitted, reducing the bandwidth and power requirements. The mathematical representation of an SSB signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_ct + k_pm(t)) - A_c\sin(2\pi f_ct + k_pm(t))
$$

where `$A_c$` is the amplitude of the carrier signal, `$f_c$` is the carrier frequency, and `$k_p$` is the phase sensitivity constant.

SSB has the advantage of reducing the bandwidth and power requirements, making it more efficient compared to other modulation techniques. However, it is more susceptible to noise and interference compared to AM, FM, and PM.





#### 2.1c Phase Modulation Techniques

Phase modulation (PM) is a type of modulation where the phase of the carrier signal is varied in accordance with the digital data being transmitted. PM is a form of angle modulation, where the amplitude of the carrier signal remains constant. This makes it particularly useful in applications where the transmitted power needs to be controlled, such as in wireless communication systems.

The mathematical representation of a PM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_ct + k_pm(t))
$$

where `$A_c$` is the amplitude of the carrier signal, `$f_c$` is the carrier frequency, and `$k_p$` is the phase modulation index. The phase modulation index is a measure of the amount of phase change per unit of time, and it is directly related to the data rate of the transmitted signal.

PM has the advantage of being able to transmit data at a higher rate compared to AM and FM. However, it is susceptible to noise and interference, which can cause distortion in the received signal.

#### 2.1c.1 Continuous Phase Modulation

Continuous phase modulation (CPM) is a method for modulation of data commonly used in wireless modems. In contrast to other coherent digital phase modulation techniques where the carrier phase abruptly resets to zero at the start of every symbol (e.g. M-PSK), with CPM the carrier phase is modulated in a continuous manner.

The primary drawback of CPM is the high implementation complexity required for an optimal receiver. This is due to the fact that the optimal receiver cannot make decisions on any isolated symbol without taking the entire sequence of transmitted symbols into account. This requires a maximum-likelihood sequence estimator (MLSE), which is efficiently implemented using the Viterbi algorithm.

#### 2.1c.2 Minimum-Shift Keying

Minimum-shift keying (MSK) is another name for CPM with an excess bandwidth of 1/2 and a linear "phase trajectory". MSK has the advantage of being able to transmit data at a higher rate compared to other modulation techniques. However, it is susceptible to noise and interference, which can cause distortion in the received signal.

In the next section, we will explore the different types of digital modulation techniques in more detail, including their advantages and disadvantages.




#### 2.1d Quadrature Modulation Techniques

Quadrature modulation is a form of modulation where two carriers, 90 degrees out of phase with each other, are used to transmit the in-phase and quadrature components of the signal. This technique is particularly useful in wireless communication systems, as it allows for the efficient transmission of both amplitude and phase information.

The mathematical representation of a quadrature modulation signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_ct + k_pm(t)) + A_c\sin(2\pi f_ct + k_pm(t))
$$

where `$A_c$` is the amplitude of the carrier signal, `$f_c$` is the carrier frequency, and `$k_p$` is the phase modulation index. The phase modulation index is a measure of the amount of phase change per unit of time, and it is directly related to the data rate of the transmitted signal.

Quadrature modulation has the advantage of being able to transmit data at a higher rate compared to single-carrier modulation techniques. However, it is susceptible to noise and interference, which can cause distortion in the received signal.

#### 2.1d.1 Quadrature Amplitude Modulation

Quadrature amplitude modulation (QAM) is a form of quadrature modulation where both the amplitude and phase of the carrier signals are varied to transmit digital data. This technique is commonly used in wireless communication systems, particularly in digital subscriber line (DSL) technology.

The mathematical representation of a QAM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_ct + k_pm(t)) + A_c\sin(2\pi f_ct + k_pm(t))
$$

where `$A_c$` is the amplitude of the carrier signal, `$f_c$` is the carrier frequency, and `$k_p$` is the phase modulation index. The phase modulation index is a measure of the amount of phase change per unit of time, and it is directly related to the data rate of the transmitted signal.

QAM has the advantage of being able to transmit data at a higher rate compared to single-carrier modulation techniques. However, it is susceptible to noise and interference, which can cause distortion in the received signal.

#### 2.1d.2 Single-Sideband Modulation

Single-sideband modulation (SSB) is a form of quadrature modulation where one of the carriers is removed, resulting in a signal with only one sideband. This technique is commonly used in amateur radio to reduce power consumption and improve signal quality.

The mathematical representation of an SSB signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = s_\text{ssb}(t) = s(t) \cdot \cos\left(2\pi f_0 t\right) - \widehat{s}(t)\cdot \sin\left(2\pi f_0 t\right)
$$

where `$s(t)$` is the message (real-valued), `$\widehat{s}(t)$` is its Hilbert transform, and `$f_0$` is the radio carrier frequency.

SSB has the advantage of being able to transmit data at a higher rate compared to single-carrier modulation techniques. However, it is susceptible to noise and interference, which can cause distortion in the received signal.

#### 2.1d.3 Differential Quadrature Modulation

Differential quadrature modulation (DQM) is a form of quadrature modulation where the phase difference between the two carriers is used to transmit digital data. This technique is commonly used in wireless communication systems, particularly in optical communication systems.

The mathematical representation of a DQM signal can be expressed as:

$$
s(t) = A_c\cos(2\pi f_ct + k_pm(t)) - A_c\sin(2\pi f_ct + k_pm(t))
$$

where `$A_c$` is the amplitude of the carrier signal, `$f_c$` is the carrier frequency, and `$k_p$` is the phase modulation index. The phase modulation index is a measure of the amount of phase change per unit of time, and it is directly related to the data rate of the transmitted signal.

DQM has the advantage of being able to transmit data at a higher rate compared to single-carrier modulation techniques. However, it is susceptible to noise and interference, which can cause distortion in the received signal.




#### 2.2a Turbo Codes

Turbo codes are a class of error control codes that were first introduced in the 1990s. They were designed to provide near-Shannon limit performance, meaning they can achieve error rates close to the theoretical limit for a given channel capacity. Turbo codes are particularly useful in wireless communication systems, where the channel conditions can vary rapidly and unpredictably.

The basic idea behind turbo codes is to use two parallel convolutional encoders and decoders, with an interleaver in between. The interleaver spreads out the information over time, which helps to reduce the effects of burst errors. The decoders then work together to decode the received signal, using the information from the other decoder to help with the decoding process.

The mathematical representation of a turbo code can be expressed as:

$$
\mathbf{y} = \mathbf{H}\mathbf{x} + \mathbf{n}
$$

where `$\mathbf{y}$` is the received signal, `$\mathbf{H}$` is the channel matrix, `$\mathbf{x}$` is the transmitted signal, and `$\mathbf{n}$` is the noise vector. The channel matrix `$\mathbf{H}$` is typically sparse, representing the fact that only a small number of channel coefficients are non-zero at any given time.

Turbo codes have been widely adopted in various communication standards, including 3G and 4G cellular networks, satellite communication, and wireless local area networks (WLANs). They have also been used in deep space communication, where the channel conditions can be extremely challenging.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the structure and operation of turbo codes, and discuss some of the key techniques used in their design and implementation.

#### 2.2b Convolutional Codes

Convolutional codes are a class of error control codes that are widely used in wireless communication systems. They are particularly useful in situations where the channel conditions can vary rapidly and unpredictably, such as in mobile communication systems.

The basic idea behind convolutional codes is to use a finite state machine to encode the information. The encoder has a set of states, and the input data is used to transition between these states. The current state of the encoder determines the output data.

The mathematical representation of a convolutional encoder can be expressed as:

$$
\mathbf{y} = \mathbf{H}\mathbf{x} + \mathbf{n}
$$

where `$\mathbf{y}$` is the received signal, `$\mathbf{H}$` is the channel matrix, `$\mathbf{x}$` is the transmitted signal, and `$\mathbf{n}$` is the noise vector. The channel matrix `$\mathbf{H}$` is typically sparse, representing the fact that only a small number of channel coefficients are non-zero at any given time.

Convolutional codes are particularly useful in situations where the channel conditions can vary rapidly and unpredictably, such as in mobile communication systems. They are also used in situations where the channel is non-linear, such as in satellite communication.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the structure and operation of convolutional codes, and discuss some of the key techniques used in their design and implementation.

#### 2.2c Low-Density Parity-Check Codes

Low-density parity-check (LDPC) codes are a class of error control codes that have gained significant attention in recent years due to their ability to achieve near-Shannon limit performance. They are particularly useful in situations where the channel conditions can vary rapidly and unpredictably, such as in wireless communication systems.

The basic idea behind LDPC codes is to use a sparse parity-check matrix to encode the information. The parity-check matrix `$\mathbf{H}$` is sparse, meaning that it has a large number of zero entries. This allows for efficient decoding, as the number of parity-check equations is much smaller than the number of information bits.

The mathematical representation of an LDPC encoder can be expressed as:

$$
\mathbf{y} = \mathbf{H}\mathbf{x} + \mathbf{n}
$$

where `$\mathbf{y}$` is the received signal, `$\mathbf{H}$` is the parity-check matrix, `$\mathbf{x}$` is the transmitted signal, and `$\mathbf{n}$` is the noise vector. The parity-check matrix `$\mathbf{H}$` is typically sparse, representing the fact that only a small number of channel coefficients are non-zero at any given time.

LDPC codes are particularly useful in situations where the channel conditions can vary rapidly and unpredictably, such as in mobile communication systems. They are also used in situations where the channel is non-linear, such as in satellite communication.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the structure and operation of LDPC codes, and discuss some of the key techniques used in their design and implementation.

#### 2.2d Polar Codes

Polar codes are a class of error control codes that have been shown to achieve the Shannon limit, making them particularly attractive for use in wireless communication systems. They are named as such because the encoding and decoding process can be visualized as a polarization of the information bits into two sets: the transmitted bits and the parity-check bits.

The basic idea behind polar codes is to use a set of parity-check equations to encode the information. The parity-check equations are generated from a set of generator polynomials, which are used to define the encoding and decoding process. The generator polynomials are typically chosen to be sparse, meaning that they have a large number of zero coefficients. This allows for efficient encoding and decoding, as the number of parity-check equations is much smaller than the number of information bits.

The mathematical representation of a polar encoder can be expressed as:

$$
\mathbf{y} = \mathbf{H}\mathbf{x} + \mathbf{n}
$$

where `$\mathbf{y}$` is the received signal, `$\mathbf{H}$` is the parity-check matrix, `$\mathbf{x}$` is the transmitted signal, and `$\mathbf{n}$` is the noise vector. The parity-check matrix `$\mathbf{H}$` is typically sparse, representing the fact that only a small number of channel coefficients are non-zero at any given time.

Polar codes are particularly useful in situations where the channel conditions can vary rapidly and unpredictably, such as in mobile communication systems. They are also used in situations where the channel is non-linear, such as in satellite communication.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the structure and operation of polar codes, and discuss some of the key techniques used in their design and implementation.

#### 2.2e Reed-Solomon Codes

Reed-Solomon (RS) codes are a class of error control codes that have been widely used in various communication systems, including wireless communication. They are named after Irving S. Reed and Gustave Solomon, who first introduced them in 1960. RS codes are particularly attractive due to their ability to correct multiple random symbol errors, making them suitable for use in situations where the channel conditions can vary rapidly and unpredictably.

The basic idea behind RS codes is to use a set of generator polynomials to encode the information. The generator polynomials are chosen such that they have a large number of zero coefficients, which allows for efficient encoding and decoding. The encoding process involves multiplying the information bits by the generator polynomials, and the decoding process involves using the syndrome decoding algorithm to correct the errors.

The mathematical representation of an RS encoder can be expressed as:

$$
\mathbf{y} = \mathbf{G}\mathbf{x} + \mathbf{n}
$$

where `$\mathbf{y}$` is the received signal, `$\mathbf{G}$` is the generator matrix, `$\mathbf{x}$` is the transmitted signal, and `$\mathbf{n}$` is the noise vector. The generator matrix `$\mathbf{G}$` is typically sparse, representing the fact that only a small number of channel coefficients are non-zero at any given time.

RS codes are particularly useful in situations where the channel conditions can vary rapidly and unpredictably, such as in mobile communication systems. They are also used in situations where the channel is non-linear, such as in satellite communication.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the structure and operation of RS codes, and discuss some of the key techniques used in their design and implementation.

The basic idea behind polar codes is to use a set of parity-check equations to encode the information. The parity-check equations are generated from a set of generator polynomials, which are used to define the encoding and decoding process. The generator polynomials are typically chosen to be sparse, meaning that they have a large number of zero coefficients. This allows for efficient encoding and decoding, as the number of parity-check equations is much smaller than the number of information bits.

The mathematical representation of a polar encoder can be expressed as:

$$
\mathbf{y} = \mathbf{H}\mathbf{x} + \mathbf{n}
$$

where `$\mathbf{y}$` is the received signal, `$\mathbf{H}$` is the parity-check matrix, `$\mathbf{x}$` is the transmitted signal, and `$\mathbf{n}$` is the noise vector. The parity-check matrix `$\mathbf{H}$` is typically sparse, representing the fact that only a small number of channel coefficients are non-zero at any given time.

Polar codes are particularly useful in situations where the channel conditions can vary rapidly and unpredictably, such as in mobile communication systems. They are also used in situations where the channel is non-linear, such as in satellite communication.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the structure and operation of polar codes, and discuss some of the key techniques used in their design and implementation.

#### 2.2q Reed-Solomon Codes

Reed-Solomon (RS) codes are a class of error control codes that have been widely used in various communication systems, including wireless communication. They are named after Irving S. Reed and Gustave Solomon, who first introduced them in 1960. RS codes are particularly attractive due to their ability to correct multiple random symbol errors, making them suitable for use in situations where the channel conditions can vary rapidly and unpredictably.

The basic idea behind RS codes is to use a set of generator polynomials to encode the information. The generator polynomials are chosen such that they have a large number of zero coefficients, which allows for efficient encoding and decoding. The encoding process involves multiplying the information bits by the generator polynomials, and the decoding process involves using the syndrome decoding algorithm to correct the errors.

The mathematical representation of an RS encoder can be expressed as:

$$
\mathbf{y} = \mathbf{G}\mathbf{x} + \mathbf{n}
$$

where `$\mathbf{y}$` is the received signal, `$\mathbf{G}$` is the generator matrix, `$\mathbf{x}$` is the transmitted signal, and `$\mathbf{n}$` is the noise vector. The generator matrix `$\mathbf{G}$` is typically sparse, representing the fact that only a small number of channel coefficients are non-zero at any given time.

RS codes are particularly useful in situations where the channel conditions can vary rapidly and unpredictably, such as in mobile communication systems. They are also used in situations where the channel is non-linear, such as in satellite communication.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the structure and operation of RS codes, and discuss some of the key techniques used in their design and implementation.

#### 2.2r Convolutional Codes

Convolutional codes are a class of error control codes that have been widely used in various communication systems, including wireless communication. They are particularly attractive due to their ability to correct burst errors, making them suitable for use in situations where the channel conditions can vary rapidly and unpredictably.

The basic idea behind convolutional codes is to use a set of generator polynomials to encode the information. The generator polynomials are chosen such that they have a large number of zero coefficients, which allows for efficient encoding and decoding. The encoding process involves multiplying the information bits by the generator polynomials, and the decoding process involves using the Viterbi algorithm to correct the errors.

The mathematical representation of a convolutional encoder can be expressed as:

$$
\mathbf{y} = \mathbf{G}\mathbf{x} + \mathbf{n}
$$

where `$\mathbf{y}$` is the received signal, `$\mathbf{G}$` is the generator matrix, `$\mathbf{x}$` is the transmitted signal, and `$\mathbf{n}$` is the noise vector. The generator matrix `$\mathbf{G}$` is typically sparse, representing the fact that only a small number of channel coefficients are non-zero at any given time.

Convolutional codes are particularly useful in situations where the channel conditions can vary rapidly and unpredictably, such as in mobile communication systems. They are also used in situations where the channel is non-linear, such as in satellite communication.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the structure and operation of convolutional codes, and discuss some of the key techniques used in their design and implementation.

#### 2.2s Low-Density Parity-Check Codes

Low-density parity-check (LDPC) codes are a class of error control codes that have gained significant attention in recent years due to their ability to achieve near-Shannon limit performance. They are particularly useful in situations where the channel conditions can vary rapidly and unpredictably, such as in wireless communication systems.

The basic idea behind LDPC codes is to use a sparse parity-check matrix to encode the information. The parity-check matrix `$\mathbf{H}$` is sparse, meaning that it has a large number of zero entries. This allows for efficient decoding, as the number of parity-check equations is much smaller than the number of information bits.

The mathematical representation of an LDPC encoder can be expressed as:

$$
\mathbf{y} = \mathbf{H}\mathbf{x} + \mathbf{n}
$$

where `$\mathbf{y}$` is the received signal, `$\mathbf{H}$` is the parity-check matrix, `$\mathbf{x}$` is the transmitted signal, and `$\mathbf{n}$` is the noise vector. The parity-check matrix `$\mathbf{H}$` is typically sparse, representing the fact that only a small number of channel coefficients are non-zero at any given time.

LDPC codes are particularly useful in situations where the channel conditions can vary rapidly and unpredictably, such as in mobile communication systems. They are also used in situations where the channel is non-linear, such as in satellite communication.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the structure and operation of LDPC codes, and discuss some of the key techniques used in their design and implementation.

#### 2.2t Polar Codes

Polar codes are a class of error control codes that have been shown to achieve the Shannon limit, making them particularly attractive for use in wireless communication systems. They are named as such because the encoding and decoding process can be visualized as a polarization of the information bits into two sets: the transmitted bits and the parity-check bits.

The basic idea behind polar codes is to use a set of parity-check equations to encode the information. The parity-check equations are generated from a set of generator polynomials, which are used to define the encoding and decoding process. The generator polynomials are typically chosen to be sparse, meaning that they have a large number of zero coefficients. This allows for efficient encoding and decoding, as the number of parity-check equations is much smaller than the number of information bits.

The mathematical representation of a polar encoder can be expressed as:

$$
\mathbf{y} = \mathbf{H}\mathbf{x} + \mathbf{n}
$$

where `$\mathbf{y}$` is the received signal, `$\mathbf{H}$` is the parity-check matrix, `$\mathbf{x}$` is the transmitted signal, and `$\mathbf{n}$` is the noise vector. The parity-check matrix `$\mathbf{H}$` is typically sparse, representing the fact that only a small number of channel coefficients are non-zero at any given time.

Polar codes are particularly useful in situations where the channel conditions can vary rapidly and unpredictably, such as in mobile communication systems. They are also used in situations where the channel is non-linear, such as in satellite communication.

#### 2.2b Convolutional Codes

Convolutional codes are a class of error control codes that are widely used in wireless communication systems. They are particularly useful in situations where the channel conditions can vary rapidly and unpredictably, such as in mobile communication systems.

The basic idea behind convolutional codes is to use a set of convolutional encoders and decoders to encode and decode the transmitted signal. The encoders and decoders are connected in a parallel structure, with an interleaver in between. The interleaver helps to spread out the information over time, which reduces the effects of burst errors.

The mathematical representation of a convolutional code can be expressed as:

$$
\mathbf{y} = \mathbf{H}\mathbf{x} + \mathbf{n}
$$

where `$\mathbf{y}$` is the received signal, `$\mathbf{H}$` is the channel matrix, `$\mathbf{x}$` is the transmitted signal, and `$\mathbf{n}$` is the noise vector. The channel matrix `$\mathbf{H}$` is typically sparse, representing the fact that only a small number of channel coefficients are non-zero at any given time.

Convolutional codes have been widely adopted in various communication standards, including 3G and 4G cellular networks, satellite communication, and wireless local area networks (WLANs). They have also been used in deep space communication, where the channel conditions can be extremely challenging.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the structure and operation of convolutional codes, and discuss some of the key techniques used in their design and implementation.

#### 2.2c Turbo Codes

Turbo codes are a class of error control codes that were first introduced in the 1990s. They are particularly useful in situations where the channel conditions can vary rapidly and unpredictably, such as in mobile communication systems.

The basic idea behind turbo codes is to use two parallel convolutional encoders and decoders, with an interleaver in between. The interleaver helps to spread out the information over time, which reduces the effects of burst errors. The decoders then work together to decode the received signal, using the information from the other decoder to help with the decoding process.

The mathematical representation of a turbo code can be expressed as:

$$
\mathbf{y} = \mathbf{H}\mathbf{x} + \mathbf{n}
$$

where `$\mathbf{y}$` is the received signal, `$\mathbf{H}$` is the channel matrix, `$\mathbf{x}$` is the transmitted signal, and `$\mathbf{n}$` is the noise vector. The channel matrix `$\mathbf{H}$` is typically sparse, representing the fact that only a small number of channel coefficients are non-zero at any given time.

Turbo codes have been widely adopted in various communication standards, including 3G and 4G cellular networks, satellite communication, and wireless local area networks (WLANs). They have also been used in deep space communication, where the channel conditions can be extremely challenging.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the structure and operation of turbo codes, and discuss some of the key techniques used in their design and implementation.

#### 2.2d LDPC Codes

Low-Density Parity-Check (LDPC) codes are a class of error control codes that were first introduced in the 1960s. They are particularly useful in situations where the channel conditions can vary rapidly and unpredictably, such as in mobile communication systems.

The basic idea behind LDPC codes is to use a set of parity-check matrices to encode and decode the transmitted signal. The parity-check matrices are sparse, meaning that they have a large number of zero entries. This sparsity allows for efficient implementation of the encoders and decoders, making LDPC codes particularly attractive for use in wireless communication systems.

The mathematical representation of an LDPC code can be expressed as:

$$
\mathbf{H}\mathbf{x} = \mathbf{n}
$$

where `$\mathbf{H}$` is the parity-check matrix, `$\mathbf{x}$` is the transmitted signal, and `$\mathbf{n}$` is the noise vector. The parity-check matrix `$\mathbf{H}$` is typically sparse, representing the fact that only a small number of channel coefficients are non-zero at any given time.

LDPC codes have been widely adopted in various communication standards, including 3G and 4G cellular networks, satellite communication, and wireless local area networks (WLANs). They have also been used in deep space communication, where the channel conditions can be extremely challenging.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the structure and operation of LDPC codes, and discuss some of the key techniques used in their design and implementation.

The encoders and decoders are connected in a parallel structure, with an interleaver in between. The interleaver helps to spread out the information over time, which reduces the effects of burst errors.

The mathematical representation of a Convolutional Code can be expressed as:

$$
\mathbf{y} = \mathbf{H}\mathbf{x} + \mathbf{n}
$$

where `$\mathbf{y}$` is the received signal, `$\mathbf{H}$` is the channel matrix, `$\mathbf{x}$` is the transmitted signal, and `$\mathbf{n}$` is the noise vector. The channel matrix `$\mathbf{H}$` is typically sparse, representing the fact that only a small number of channel coefficients are non-zero at any given time.

Convolutional Codes have been widely adopted in various communication standards, including 3G and 4G cellular networks, satellite communication, and wireless local area networks (WLANs). They have also been used in deep space communication, where the channel conditions can be extremely challenging.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the structure and operation of Convolutional Codes, and discuss some of the key techniques used in their design and implementation.

#### 2.2s Turbo Codes

Turbo Codes are a class of error control codes that were first introduced in the 1990s. They are particularly useful in situations where the channel conditions can vary rapidly and unpredictably, such as in mobile communication systems.

The basic idea behind Turbo Codes is to use two parallel convolutional encoders and decoders, with an interleaver in between. The interleaver helps to spread out the information over time, which reduces the effects of burst errors. The decoders then work together to decode the received signal, using the information from the other decoder to help with the decoding process.

The mathematical representation of a Turbo Code can be expressed as:

$$
\mathbf{y} = \mathbf{H}\mathbf{x} + \mathbf{n}
$$

where `$\mathbf{y}$` is the received signal, `$\mathbf{H}$` is the channel matrix, `$\mathbf{x}$` is the transmitted signal, and `$\mathbf{n}$` is the noise vector. The channel matrix `$\mathbf{H}$` is typically sparse, representing the fact that only a small number of channel coefficients are non-zero at any given time.

Turbo Codes have been widely adopted in various communication standards, including 3G and 4G cellular networks, satellite communication, and wireless local area networks (WLANs). They have also been used in deep space communication, where the channel conditions can be extremely challenging.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the structure and operation of Turbo Codes, and discuss some of the key techniques used in their design and implementation.

#### 2.2t LDPC Codes

Low-Density Parity-Check (LDPC) Codes are a class of error control codes that were first introduced in the 1960s. They are particularly useful in situations where the channel conditions can vary rapidly and unpredictably, such as in mobile communication systems.

The basic idea behind LDPC Codes is to use a set of parity-check matrices to encode and decode the transmitted signal. The parity-check matrices are sparse, meaning that they have a large number of zero entries. This sparsity allows for efficient implementation of the encoders and decoders, making LDPC Codes particularly attractive for use in wireless communication systems.

The mathematical representation of an LDPC Code can be expressed as:

$$
\mathbf{y} = \mathbf{H}\mathbf{x} + \mathbf{n}
$$

where `$\mathbf{y}$` is the received signal, `$\mathbf{H}$` is the parity-check matrix, `$\mathbf{x}$` is the transmitted signal, and `$\mathbf{n}$` is the noise vector. The parity-check matrix `$\mathbf{H}$` is typically sparse, representing the fact that only a small number of channel coefficients are non-zero at any given time.

LDPC Codes have been widely adopted in various communication standards, including 3G and 4G cellular networks, satellite communication, and wireless local area networks (WLANs). They have also been used in deep space communication, where the channel conditions can be extremely challenging.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the structure and operation of LDPC Codes, and discuss some of the key techniques used in their design and implementation.

#### 2.2u Polar Codes

Polar Codes are a class of error control codes that were first introduced in 2009. They are particularly useful in situations where the channel conditions can vary rapidly and unpredictably, such as in mobile communication systems.

The basic idea behind Polar Codes is to use a set of parity-check matrices to encode and decode the transmitted signal. The parity-check matrices are constructed from the powers of a generator matrix, which is a sparse matrix. This sparsity allows for efficient implementation of the encoders and decoders, making Polar Codes particularly attractive for use in wireless communication systems.

The mathematical representation of a Polar Code can be expressed as:

$$
\mathbf{y} = \mathbf{H}\mathbf{x} + \mathbf{n}
$$

where `$\mathbf{y}$` is the received signal, `$\mathbf{H}$` is the parity-check matrix, `$\mathbf{x}$` is the transmitted signal, and `$\mathbf{n}$` is the noise vector. The parity-check matrix `$\mathbf{H}$` is typically sparse, representing the fact that only a small number of channel coefficients are non-zero at any given time.

Polar Codes have been widely adopted in various communication standards, including 3G and 4G cellular networks, satellite communication, and wireless local area networks (WLANs). They have also been used in deep space communication, where the channel conditions can be extremely challenging.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the structure and operation of Polar Codes, and discuss some of the key techniques used in their design and implementation.

#### 2.2c Channel Coding Theorems

Channel coding theorems are fundamental to the design and analysis of error control codes. They provide theoretical limits on the performance of these codes, and guide the design of practical codes that approach these limits. In this section, we will discuss some of the key channel coding theorems, including the Shannon-Fano theorem, the Shannon-McMillan theorem, and the Gallager bound.

##### Shannon-Fano Theorem

The Shannon-Fano theorem is a fundamental result in information theory that provides a lower bound on the entropy of a random variable. It is named after Claude Shannon and Robert Fano, who first introduced it in the 1940s.

The theorem states that for a random variable $X$ with alphabet $\mathcal{X}$ and probability distribution $p(x)$, the entropy $H(X)$ is lower bounded by:

$$
H(X) \geq \frac{1}{\log(2)} \sum_{x \in \mathcal{X}} p(x) \log(p(x))
$$

This theorem is particularly useful in the design of error control codes, as it provides a lower bound on the minimum rate at which information can be reliably transmitted over a noisy channel.

##### Shannon-McMillan Theorem

The Shannon-McMillan theorem is another fundamental result in information theory, named after Claude Shannon and John McMillan. It provides an upper bound on the entropy of a random variable, and is particularly useful in the design of error control codes.

The theorem states that for a random variable $X$ with alphabet $\mathcal{X}$ and probability distribution $p(x)$, the entropy $H(X)$ is upper bounded by:

$$
H(X) \leq \frac{1}{\log(2)} \sum_{x \in \mathcal{X}} p(x) \log(p(x)) + \frac{1}{\log(2)} \sum_{x \in \mathcal{X}} p(x) \log(1/p(x))
$$

This theorem is particularly useful in the design of error control codes, as it provides an upper bound on the maximum rate at which information can be reliably transmitted over a noisy channel.

##### Gallager Bound

The Gallager bound is a result in information theory named after Robert Gallager. It provides an upper bound on the probability of error for a binary symmetric channel, and is particularly useful in the design of error control codes.

The bound states that for a binary symmetric channel with crossover probability $p$, the probability of error $P_e$ is upper bounded by:

$$
P_e \leq \frac{1}{2} \left( 1 - \sqrt{1 - \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^{n} (1 - 2p)^i} \right)
$$

This bound is particularly useful in the design of error control codes, as it provides a theoretical limit on the performance of these codes.




#### 2.2d Error Correction Techniques

Error correction techniques are a set of methods used to detect and correct errors that occur during the transmission of information. These techniques are essential in wireless communications, where the signal is often subject to various forms of interference and noise. In this section, we will discuss some of the key error correction techniques, including the Reed-Solomon codes, the Hamming codes, and the convolutional codes.

##### Reed-Solomon Codes

Reed-Solomon (RS) codes are a family of error-correcting codes that are particularly well-suited to correcting burst errors. They were first introduced by Irving S. Reed and Gustave Solomon in 1960.

The basic idea behind RS codes is to encode a message into a set of polynomials, and then to transmit these polynomials over a noisy channel. The receiver can then use the syndromes of these polynomials to detect and correct errors.

The encoding process involves evaluating the message polynomial at a set of points, known as the generator points. The resulting values are then transmitted over the channel.

The decoding process involves computing the syndromes of the received values. If the syndromes are zero, then no errors have occurred. If the syndromes are non-zero, then an error has occurred, and the decoder can use the syndromes to determine the location and value of the error.

##### Hamming Codes

Hamming codes are a family of linear error-correcting codes. They were first introduced by Richard Hamming in 1950.

The basic idea behind Hamming codes is to add redundant bits to the message, known as parity check bits. These bits are calculated based on the original message bits, and are used to detect and correct single-bit errors.

The encoding process involves adding parity check bits to the message. The resulting codeword is then transmitted over the channel.

The decoding process involves computing the parity of the received codeword. If the parity is even, then no errors have occurred. If the parity is odd, then an error has occurred, and the decoder can use the parity to determine the location and value of the error.

##### Convolutional Codes

Convolutional codes are a family of error-correcting codes that are particularly well-suited to correcting random errors. They were first introduced by Peter Elias in 1955.

The basic idea behind convolutional codes is to encode a message into a set of shift registers, and then to transmit these shift registers over a noisy channel. The receiver can then use the syndromes of these shift registers to detect and correct errors.

The encoding process involves shifting the message through a set of shift registers. The resulting values are then transmitted over the channel.

The decoding process involves computing the syndromes of the received values. If the syndromes are zero, then no errors have occurred. If the syndromes are non-zero, then an error has occurred, and the decoder can use the syndromes to determine the location and value of the error.




#### 2.3a Shannon Capacity

The Shannon Capacity, named after Claude Shannon, is a fundamental concept in information theory that quantifies the maximum rate at which information can be transmitted over a noisy channel. It is a key concept in the study of channel capacity and is named after Claude Shannon, who first introduced it in his seminal 1948 paper.

The Shannon Capacity is defined as the maximum mutual information between the input and output of a channel, given the channel's noise characteristics. In other words, it is the maximum amount of information that can be reliably transmitted over the channel, given the channel's noise characteristics.

The Shannon Capacity is given by the formula:

$$
C = \max_{p(x)} I(X;Y)
$$

where $C$ is the Shannon Capacity, $p(x)$ is the probability distribution of the input $X$, and $I(X;Y)$ is the mutual information between $X$ and $Y$.

The Shannon Capacity is a key concept in the study of channel capacity, as it provides a theoretical upper bound on the rate at which information can be transmitted over a noisy channel. It is also a key concept in the study of modulation and coding, as it provides a theoretical limit on the rate at which information can be transmitted over a noisy channel, given the channel's noise characteristics.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of channel capacity in more detail, and explore its implications for modulation and coding.

#### 2.3b Channel Capacity and Modulation

The concept of channel capacity is closely tied to the concept of modulation. Modulation is a process that allows information to be transmitted over a communication channel by varying one or more properties of a carrier signal. The properties of the carrier signal that are varied are known as the modulating signals.

The channel capacity of a modulated channel is determined by the modulation scheme used. Different modulation schemes have different channel capacities. For example, the channel capacity of a binary symmetric channel is given by the Shannon Capacity, which is the maximum mutual information between the input and output of the channel, given the channel's noise characteristics.

The channel capacity of a modulated channel can be calculated using the formula:

$$
C = \max_{p(x)} I(X;Y)
$$

where $C$ is the channel capacity, $p(x)$ is the probability distribution of the input $X$, and $I(X;Y)$ is the mutual information between $X$ and $Y$.

The channel capacity of a modulated channel can be increased by using a modulation scheme that allows for a larger number of possible input symbols. This is because the mutual information between the input and output of the channel is proportional to the logarithm of the number of possible input symbols. Therefore, by increasing the number of possible input symbols, the mutual information can be increased, and hence the channel capacity can be increased.

However, increasing the number of possible input symbols also increases the complexity of the modulation scheme. This can make the modulation scheme more difficult to implement and can also increase the susceptibility of the modulation scheme to errors. Therefore, there is a trade-off between the channel capacity and the complexity of the modulation scheme.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of coding and how it can be used to increase the channel capacity of a modulated channel.

#### 2.3c Channel Capacity and Coding

Coding is another crucial aspect of wireless communications that directly impacts the channel capacity. Coding is the process of adding redundancy to the information signal to enable error detection and correction. The redundancy is introduced by encoding the information into a code, which is a set of codewords. Each codeword represents a possible value of the information signal.

The channel capacity of a coded channel is determined by the coding scheme used. Different coding schemes have different channel capacities. The channel capacity of a coded channel can be calculated using the formula:

$$
C = \max_{p(x)} I(X;Y) - H(Y)
$$

where $C$ is the channel capacity, $p(x)$ is the probability distribution of the input $X$, $I(X;Y)$ is the mutual information between $X$ and $Y$, and $H(Y)$ is the entropy of the output $Y$.

The channel capacity of a coded channel can be increased by using a coding scheme that allows for a larger number of possible codewords. This is because the mutual information between the input and output of the channel is proportional to the logarithm of the number of possible codewords. Therefore, by increasing the number of possible codewords, the mutual information can be increased, and hence the channel capacity can be increased.

However, increasing the number of possible codewords also increases the complexity of the coding scheme. This can make the coding scheme more difficult to implement and can also increase the susceptibility of the coding scheme to errors. Therefore, there is a trade-off between the channel capacity and the complexity of the coding scheme.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of modulation and coding together, and how they can be combined to achieve the maximum channel capacity.

#### 2.3d Channel Capacity and Noise

Noise is an inherent part of any communication channel, and it is a significant factor that affects the channel capacity. Noise is any unwanted disturbance that interferes with the transmission of information. It can be caused by various sources, including thermal noise, interference from other signals, and imperfections in the communication system.

The presence of noise in a channel reduces the channel capacity. This is because noise increases the uncertainty in the received signal, which reduces the mutual information between the input and output of the channel. The channel capacity can be calculated using the formula:

$$
C = \max_{p(x)} I(X;Y) - H(Y) - H(N)
$$

where $C$ is the channel capacity, $p(x)$ is the probability distribution of the input $X$, $I(X;Y)$ is the mutual information between $X$ and $Y$, $H(Y)$ is the entropy of the output $Y$, and $H(N)$ is the entropy of the noise $N$.

The channel capacity can be increased by reducing the noise in the channel. This can be achieved by using error correction codes, which add redundancy to the information signal and enable error detection and correction. The redundancy allows the receiver to correct some of the errors caused by the noise, which reduces the uncertainty in the received signal and increases the mutual information.

However, reducing the noise also increases the complexity of the communication system. This is because error correction codes require more complex decoding algorithms, and they also increase the power consumption. Therefore, there is a trade-off between the channel capacity and the complexity of the communication system.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of modulation and coding together, and how they can be combined to achieve the maximum channel capacity in the presence of noise.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fundamental concepts of modulation and coding, two critical components of wireless communications. We have explored the principles behind modulation, a process that allows information to be transmitted over a communication channel. We have also examined the role of coding in wireless communications, particularly in the context of error correction and detection.

We have learned that modulation is the process of modifying a carrier signal with the information signal to transmit the information. We have also discovered that coding is a technique used to add redundancy to the information signal, which helps in detecting and correcting errors during transmission.

The chapter has also highlighted the importance of these concepts in the field of wireless communications. Modulation and coding are essential for efficient and reliable communication over wireless channels, which are inherently prone to noise and interference.

In conclusion, understanding modulation and coding is crucial for anyone working in the field of wireless communications. These concepts form the backbone of wireless communication systems, enabling the efficient and reliable transmission of information over wireless channels.

## Chapter: Wireless Networks

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 3: Wireless Networks. This chapter delves into the fascinating world of wireless networks, a critical component of modern communication systems. Wireless networks, as the name suggests, are networks that operate without the need for physical wires. They are ubiquitous in our daily lives, from our homes and offices to public spaces like airports and parks.

In this chapter, we will explore the principles that govern the operation of wireless networks. We will start by understanding the basic concepts of wireless networks, including the different types of wireless networks and their applications. We will then delve into the technical aspects of wireless networks, discussing the various protocols and technologies that make them possible.

We will also explore the challenges and opportunities in wireless networks. Despite their many advantages, wireless networks face several challenges, including interference, security, and scalability. We will discuss these challenges and explore potential solutions. On the other hand, the rapid advancements in technology have opened up new opportunities for wireless networks, such as the Internet of Things (IoT) and 5G.

Throughout this chapter, we will use mathematical expressions to explain the principles of wireless networks. For example, we might use the equation `$y_j(n)$` to represent the output of a wireless network at time `n`. We will also use diagrams to illustrate these concepts.

By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of the principles of wireless networks and be able to apply this knowledge to real-world scenarios. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a professional in the field of wireless communications, this chapter will provide you with the knowledge and tools you need to navigate the complex world of wireless networks.




#### 2.3b Capacity Bounds

The Shannon Capacity provides a theoretical upper bound on the rate at which information can be transmitted over a noisy channel. However, in practical communication systems, it is often necessary to consider more realistic bounds on channel capacity. These bounds take into account the limitations of the communication system, such as the finite bandwidth of the channel and the finite power available for transmission.

One such bound is the Shannon Capacity with Power Constraint, which takes into account the finite power available for transmission. It is given by the formula:

$$
C = \max_{p(x)} I(X;Y) - \frac{1}{2} \log(2\pi e R)
$$

where $C$ is the Shannon Capacity with Power Constraint, $p(x)$ is the probability distribution of the input $X$, $I(X;Y)$ is the mutual information between $X$ and $Y$, and $R$ is the power constraint.

Another important bound is the Shannon Capacity with Bandwidth Constraint, which takes into account the finite bandwidth of the channel. It is given by the formula:

$$
C = \max_{p(x)} I(X;Y) - \frac{1}{2} \log(2\pi e B)
$$

where $C$ is the Shannon Capacity with Bandwidth Constraint, $p(x)$ is the probability distribution of the input $X$, $I(X;Y)$ is the mutual information between $X$ and $Y$, and $B$ is the bandwidth constraint.

These bounds provide a more realistic estimate of the channel capacity, taking into account the practical limitations of the communication system. They are important tools in the design and analysis of wireless communication systems.

#### 2.3c Capacity of Various Channels

The capacity of a channel refers to the maximum rate at which information can be reliably transmitted over the channel. It is a fundamental concept in information theory and is closely related to the concepts of modulation and coding. In this section, we will explore the capacity of various channels, including the additive white Gaussian noise (AWGN) channel, the fading channel, and the multiple-input multiple-output (MIMO) channel.

##### Additive White Gaussian Noise (AWGN) Channel

The AWGN channel is a simple yet important model in information theory. It assumes that the received signal is the sum of the transmitted signal and additive white Gaussian noise. The capacity of the AWGN channel is given by the Shannon Capacity formula:

$$
C = \max_{p(x)} I(X;Y) - \frac{1}{2} \log(2\pi e R)
$$

where $C$ is the channel capacity, $p(x)$ is the probability distribution of the input $X$, $I(X;Y)$ is the mutual information between $X$ and $Y$, and $R$ is the power constraint.

##### Fading Channel

In a fading channel, the received signal is affected by multipath propagation, which causes the signal to arrive at the receiver via multiple paths. This can lead to interference and reduce the channel capacity. However, with the use of advanced modulation and coding techniques, it is possible to achieve near-Shannon Capacity in fading channels.

##### Multiple-Input Multiple-Output (MIMO) Channel

The MIMO channel is a type of channel where multiple antennas are used at both the transmitter and receiver. This can significantly increase the channel capacity, especially in the presence of fading. The capacity of the MIMO channel is given by the formula:

$$
C = \max_{p(x)} I(X;Y) - \frac{1}{2} \log(2\pi e B)
$$

where $C$ is the channel capacity, $p(x)$ is the probability distribution of the input $X$, $I(X;Y)$ is the mutual information between $X$ and $Y$, and $B$ is the bandwidth constraint.

In the next section, we will explore how these channel capacities can be achieved in practical communication systems.




#### 2.3c Capacity of MIMO Channels

The multiple-input multiple-output (MIMO) channel is a type of wireless channel where multiple antennas are used at both the transmitter and receiver. This allows for the transmission of multiple data streams simultaneously, increasing the channel capacity and improving the reliability of communication.

The capacity of a MIMO channel can be calculated using the concept of channel matrix, which is a matrix representation of the channel. The channel matrix $H$ is defined as:

$$
H = \begin{bmatrix}
h_{11} & h_{12} & \cdots & h_{1N} \\
h_{21} & h_{22} & \cdots & h_{2N} \\
\vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \vdots \\
h_{M1} & h_{M2} & \cdots & h_{MN}
\end{bmatrix}
$$

where $h_{mn}$ is the channel response from the $m$-th transmit antenna to the $n$-th receive antenna.

The capacity of a MIMO channel can be calculated using the following formula:

$$
C = \log\det(I + \frac{P}{N_0}HH^H)
$$

where $P$ is the total power available for transmission, $N_0$ is the noise power spectral density, and $H^H$ is the conjugate transpose of the channel matrix.

This formula shows that the capacity of a MIMO channel is directly proportional to the determinant of the channel matrix. This means that the more antennas used, the higher the channel capacity. However, the capacity also depends on the channel matrix itself, which can be affected by factors such as fading and interference.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of MIMO coding, which is a technique used to improve the reliability of communication in MIMO channels.




#### 2.3d Capacity of Fading Channels

Fading is a phenomenon in wireless communication where the channel response between the transmitter and receiver changes over time. This can be caused by various factors such as multipath propagation, where the signal reaches the receiver through multiple paths, each with a different delay and attenuation. Fading can significantly affect the performance of wireless communication systems, especially in high-speed scenarios where the channel response can change rapidly.

The capacity of a fading channel is a measure of the maximum rate at which information can be reliably transmitted over the channel. It is a function of the channel response, the available power, and the noise power spectral density. The capacity of a fading channel can be calculated using the following formula:

$$
C = \max_{p(x)} I(X;Y)
$$

where $p(x)$ is the probability distribution of the input signal $X$, and $I(X;Y)$ is the mutual information between the input and output signals.

The mutual information is given by:

$$
I(X;Y) = H(Y) - H(Y|X)
$$

where $H(Y)$ is the entropy of the output signal $Y$, and $H(Y|X)$ is the conditional entropy of $Y$ given $X$.

The capacity of a fading channel is a key parameter in the design of wireless communication systems. It provides a theoretical upper bound on the rate of information transmission, and can be used to evaluate the performance of different modulation and coding schemes.

In the next section, we will discuss some techniques for dealing with fading in wireless communication systems.

#### 2.3e Capacity of Relay Channels

Relay channels are a type of wireless channel where a relay node is used to assist in the transmission of information from the source to the destination. The relay node can amplify and forward the signal, or decode and re-encode the signal before forwarding it. This can be particularly useful in scenarios where the direct link between the source and destination is not available or is subject to severe fading.

The capacity of a relay channel is a measure of the maximum rate at which information can be reliably transmitted from the source to the destination. It is a function of the channel responses between the source and relay, and between the relay and destination. The capacity of a relay channel can be calculated using the following formula:

$$
C = \max_{p(x)} I(X;Y)
$$

where $p(x)$ is the probability distribution of the input signal $X$, and $I(X;Y)$ is the mutual information between the input and output signals.

The mutual information is given by:

$$
I(X;Y) = H(Y) - H(Y|X)
$$

where $H(Y)$ is the entropy of the output signal $Y$, and $H(Y|X)$ is the conditional entropy of $Y$ given $X$.

The capacity of a relay channel is a key parameter in the design of wireless communication systems. It provides a theoretical upper bound on the rate of information transmission, and can be used to evaluate the performance of different relaying schemes.

In the next section, we will discuss some techniques for dealing with fading in relay channels.

#### 2.3f Capacity of Interference Channels

Interference channels are a type of wireless channel where multiple users transmit and receive signals simultaneously, causing interference to each other. This can be a significant challenge in wireless communication systems, especially in scenarios where the users are close to each other and the signals overlap in time and frequency.

The capacity of an interference channel is a measure of the maximum rate at which information can be reliably transmitted from all users to their respective destinations. It is a function of the channel responses between all pairs of users, and is a key parameter in the design of wireless communication systems.

The capacity of an interference channel can be calculated using the following formula:

$$
C = \sum_{i=1}^{N} \max_{p(x_i)} I(X_i;Y_i)
$$

where $p(x_i)$ is the probability distribution of the input signal $X_i$ from user $i$, and $I(X_i;Y_i)$ is the mutual information between the input and output signals of user $i$.

The mutual information is given by:

$$
I(X_i;Y_i) = H(Y_i) - H(Y_i|X_i)
$$

where $H(Y_i)$ is the entropy of the output signal $Y_i$ of user $i$, and $H(Y_i|X_i)$ is the conditional entropy of $Y_i$ given $X_i$.

The capacity of an interference channel provides a theoretical upper bound on the rate of information transmission from all users. However, achieving this capacity in practice can be challenging due to the interference between users. Various techniques, such as interference cancellation and multiple-input multiple-output (MIMO) systems, have been developed to mitigate the interference and improve the performance of interference channels.

In the next section, we will discuss some of these techniques in more detail.

#### 2.3g Capacity of MIMO Channels

Multiple-Input Multiple-Output (MIMO) channels are a type of wireless channel where multiple antennas are used at both the transmitter and receiver. This can significantly increase the capacity of the channel, especially in scenarios where the channel is subject to fading or interference.

The capacity of a MIMO channel is a measure of the maximum rate at which information can be reliably transmitted from the transmitter to the receiver. It is a function of the channel responses between all pairs of antennas, and is a key parameter in the design of wireless communication systems.

The capacity of a MIMO channel can be calculated using the following formula:

$$
C = \sum_{i=1}^{N} \max_{p(x_i)} I(X_i;Y_i)
$$

where $p(x_i)$ is the probability distribution of the input signal $X_i$ from antenna $i$, and $I(X_i;Y_i)$ is the mutual information between the input and output signals of antenna $i$.

The mutual information is given by:

$$
I(X_i;Y_i) = H(Y_i) - H(Y_i|X_i)
$$

where $H(Y_i)$ is the entropy of the output signal $Y_i$ of antenna $i$, and $H(Y_i|X_i)$ is the conditional entropy of $Y_i$ given $X_i$.

The capacity of a MIMO channel provides a theoretical upper bound on the rate of information transmission from all antennas. However, achieving this capacity in practice can be challenging due to the interference between antennas. Various techniques, such as precoding and post-processing, have been developed to mitigate the interference and improve the performance of MIMO channels.

In the next section, we will discuss some of these techniques in more detail.

#### 2.3h Capacity of OFDM Channels

Orthogonal Frequency Division Multiplexing (OFDM) is a modulation scheme that divides the available bandwidth into multiple subcarriers, each of which carries a lower rate data stream. This allows for the efficient use of the available bandwidth and can significantly increase the capacity of the channel.

The capacity of an OFDM channel is a measure of the maximum rate at which information can be reliably transmitted from the transmitter to the receiver. It is a function of the channel responses between all pairs of subcarriers, and is a key parameter in the design of wireless communication systems.

The capacity of an OFDM channel can be calculated using the following formula:

$$
C = \sum_{i=1}^{N} \max_{p(x_i)} I(X_i;Y_i)
$$

where $p(x_i)$ is the probability distribution of the input signal $X_i$ from subcarrier $i$, and $I(X_i;Y_i)$ is the mutual information between the input and output signals of subcarrier $i$.

The mutual information is given by:

$$
I(X_i;Y_i) = H(Y_i) - H(Y_i|X_i)
$$

where $H(Y_i)$ is the entropy of the output signal $Y_i$ of subcarrier $i$, and $H(Y_i|X_i)$ is the conditional entropy of $Y_i$ given $X_i$.

The capacity of an OFDM channel provides a theoretical upper bound on the rate of information transmission from all subcarriers. However, achieving this capacity in practice can be challenging due to the interference between subcarriers. Various techniques, such as filter-bank equalization and subcarrier permutation, have been developed to mitigate the interference and improve the performance of OFDM channels.

In the next section, we will discuss some of these techniques in more detail.

#### 2.3i Capacity of Relay Channels

Relay channels are a type of wireless channel where a relay node is used to assist in the transmission of information from the source to the destination. The relay node can amplify and forward the signal, or decode and re-encode the signal before forwarding it. This can be particularly useful in scenarios where the direct link between the source and destination is not available or is subject to severe fading.

The capacity of a relay channel is a measure of the maximum rate at which information can be reliably transmitted from the source to the destination. It is a function of the channel responses between the source, relay, and destination, and is a key parameter in the design of wireless communication systems.

The capacity of a relay channel can be calculated using the following formula:

$$
C = \max_{p(x)} I(X;Y)
$$

where $p(x)$ is the probability distribution of the input signal $X$, and $I(X;Y)$ is the mutual information between the input and output signals.

The mutual information is given by:

$$
I(X;Y) = H(Y) - H(Y|X)
$$

where $H(Y)$ is the entropy of the output signal $Y$, and $H(Y|X)$ is the conditional entropy of $Y$ given $X$.

The capacity of a relay channel provides a theoretical upper bound on the rate of information transmission from the source to the destination. However, achieving this capacity in practice can be challenging due to the interference between the source and destination signals, and the potential for errors in the relaying process. Various techniques, such as amplify-and-forward and decode-and-forward, have been developed to mitigate these challenges and improve the performance of relay channels.

In the next section, we will discuss some of these techniques in more detail.
